Gordana Dodig-Crnkovic
In Christian Calude (ed.), Randomness & Complexity, From Leibniz to Chaitin. World Scientific Pub Co (2007)   Copy   BIBTEX
Abstract
This essay presents arguments for the claim that in the best of all possible worlds (Leibniz) there are sources of unpredictability and creativity for us humans, even given a pancomputational stance. A suggested answer to Chaitinâs questions: âWhere do new mathematical and biological ideas come from? How do they emerge?â is that they come from the world and emerge from basic physical (computational) laws. For humans as a tiny subset of the universe, a part of the new ideas comes as the result of the re-configuration and reshaping of already existing elements and another part comes from the outside world as a consequence of openness and interactivity of biological and cognitive systems. For the universe at large it is randomness that is the source of unpredictability on the fundamental level. In order to be able to completely predict the Universe-computer we would need the Universe-computer itself to compute its next state. As Chaitin demonstrated there are incompressible truths, which means truths that cannot be computed by any other computer but the universe itself1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn her essay, "Epistemology as Computation (Information Processing)", Gordana Dodig-Crnkovic argues that unpredictability and creativity for humans exist, even from a pancomputational viewpoint, wherein everything in the universe is regarded as computation. Drawing from the philosophical concepts of Leibniz and Chaitin, she proposes that new ideas, whether mathematical or biological, arise from the fundamental computational laws of the world.Two sources contribute to the emergence of new ideas: reconfiguration and reshaping of pre-existing elements, and influences from the outside world due to the openness and interactivity of biological and cognitive systems.At the grand scale of the universe, randomness serves as the fundamental source of unpredictability. Dodig-Crnkovic suggests that to fully predict the universe's future state, we would need the universe itself to calculate it. This concept aligns with Chaitin's incompressible truths â truths that cannot be computed by any entity other than the universe itself. In essence, her argument underscores the inherent limitations in our capacity to predict or understand the universe using computation or information processing, as some truths are computable only by the universe itself.1 / 1Computationalism in Philosophy of Cognitive Science

Keywords
creativity  infocomputation  pancomputationalism  cognition1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe terms mentioned refer to concepts and themes in the philosophy of mind, cognitive science, epistemology, and philosophy of computing and information:Computationalism in Philosophy of Cognitive Science: This is the view that cognitionâthe process by which the brain understands and makes decisionsâis essentially computational. In other words, the brain operates like a computer, taking in information (input), processing it, and producing responses (output).Creativity in Philosophy of Mind: This explores the nature of creativity from a philosophical perspective. How does the mind generate new, original, and valuable ideas or solutions? What processes are involved, and how can they be explained philosophically? Creativity often challenges traditional computational models of cognition due to its unpredictable and innovative nature.Epistemic Constructivism in Epistemology: This is the view that knowledge is constructed by the individual, not passively received from the world. Rather than believing that we can have direct access to reality as it truly is, epistemic constructivists argue that our understanding of the world is shaped by our perceptual and cognitive processes.Pancomputationalism in Philosophy of Computing and Information: This is the idea that the universe itself is a computational structure, and all processes within itâincluding physical, biological, and mental onesâcan be understood as computations. This view extends the idea of computationalism beyond just the brain or cognition to everything in existence.These keywords suggest a philosophical exploration of how the universe and our cognition work from a computational perspective, highlighting the role of creativity and the concept of constructed knowledge within this computational universe.1 / 1The Problem of Pancomputationalism: 
Focusing on Three Related Arguments* 
SeongSoo Park 
Department of Philosophy, Sungkyunkwan University 
seongsoo@buffalo.edu 
Abstract 
Pancomputationalism is the view that everything is a computer. This, if 
true, poses some difficulties to the computational theory of cognition. In 
particular, the strongest version of it suggested by John Searle seems enough 
to trivialize computational cognitivistsâ core idea on which our cognitive 
system is a computing system. The aim of this paper is to argue against Searleâs 
pancomputationalism. To achieve this, I will draw a line between realized 
computers and unrealized computers. Through this distinction, I expect that it 
will become evident that Searleâs pancomputationalism should be understood in 
terms of unrealized computers, while the computational theory of cognition is 
concerned with realized computers. 
Keywords: Pancomputationalism, Computational Theory of Cognition, John Searle, 
The Chinese Room Argument, Functionalism 
1. Introduction 
The computational theory of cognition (or CTC for short) propounds that

cognition is computation. On this view, a cognitive system can be explained 
in terms of the computations it performs, because cognitive processes are 
computational. Therefore, proponents of this view (e.g., McCulloch & Pitts, 
1943; Newell & Simon, 1976; Pylyshyn, 1985; Edelman, 2008; Schneider, 
2011) claim that the human cognitive system implements (or executes) 
cognitive programs that allow us to think and make decisions. 
One of the most pertinent challenges to CTC is the so-called âstrongest 
version of pancomputationalismâ suggested by John Searle.1 In his 1990 
paper, âIs the Brain a Digital Computer?â Searle argues that everything is 
a computing system that performs every computation. According to him, 
even a wall is a computational object that can perform every program. This 
claim, if true, raises a hard problem for CTC because it trivializes CTC.

In this paper, I argue in favor of CTC, addressing three related arguments 
regarding pancomputationalism. Specifically, I argue that although the 
Chinese Room Argument is convincing, there are good reasons not to accept 
the Pan Argument. To illustrate this, I will draw a line between realized 
computers and unrealized computers. Through this distinction, I expect 
that it will become evident that Searleâs pancomputationalism, if properly 
understood, does not provide any adequate reason to deny CTC since it 
should be understood in terms of unrealized computers, rather than realized 
computers.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn his paper, SeongSoo Park explores the philosophical concept of pancomputationalism, which posits that everything is a computer. Park specifically addresses John Searle's "strongest version" of pancomputationalism and its implications for the computational theory of cognition (CTC), which proposes that cognition, and therefore our cognitive systems, can be explained as a form of computation.Searle's version of pancomputationalism suggests that every object, including inanimate objects like a wall, can perform every possible computation. This idea, if accurate, undermines CTC by trivializing it â if everything computes, then the unique computation attributed to cognition becomes less significant.To challenge Searle's pancomputationalism, Park suggests a differentiation between 'realized computers' and 'unrealized computers.' He contends that Searle's pancomputationalism should be associated with unrealized computers, while CTC deals with realized computers.Park's argument essentially focuses on refuting Searle's pancomputationalism by illustrating that it doesn't provide a substantial reason to dismiss CTC. His differentiation between realized and unrealized computers helps preserve the significant computational nature of cognition.1 / 12. The Triviality Argument 
Searle (1990) attempts to argue against CTC by endorsing the strongest 
version of pancomputationalism, according to which everything 
computes any program. However, someone might say, âSo, why does 
pancomputationalism matter to computational cognitivists?â This seems 
to be a natural reaction in that almost all computational cognitivists (e.g., 
1 Putnam also made a similar claim in his 1988 paper. 
351The 
Problem of Pancomputationalism: Focusing on Three Related Arguments 
Smart, 1959; Fodor, 1968; Newall & Simon, 1976; Pylyshyn, 1984; Horgan 
& Woodward, 1985) accept the multiple realizability thesis of computing 
objects, which propounds that there can be various types of computers that 
are comprised of different physical materials, since they gladly admit that 
computation can be functionally def i ned. 
Despite its seemingly favorable appearance, the claim that everything is 
a kind of computer that can implement (or execute) any program, if true, 
poses several serious problems for CTC. One problem is that it plays a 
crucial role within the Triviality Argument.2 The argument can be formally 
represented as follows: 
1. The computational theory of cognition states that our cognitive system 
is a computing system. 
2. Everything is a computing system that performs any computation. 
3. If (1) and (2) are true, then the computational theory of cognition is not 
informative. 
4. Therefore, the computational theory of cognition is not informative. 
Please note that the aim of the Triviality Argument is not to refute CTC. 
Rather, the argument aims to illustrate that CTC says little about how our 
cognitive system works or how it produces mental phenomena. In other 
words, the key point of the Triviality Argument is that if the strongest 
version of pancomputationalism is true, then there is nothing special in the 
claim that human cognitive processes are computational. 
Before we go through the argument, it is also worth noting that 
numerous versions of pancomputationalism exist. Not all versions of 
pancomputationalism make CTC trivial. For instance, a weaker one, which propounds that everything is a computing system that implements some 
computation, does not harm CTC. This is because CTC may secure its own 
explanatory power by arguing that our cognitive system implements a 
unique program that other computers are not able to execute.3 
It is my belief that the only way to refute the Triviality Argument is to 
deny the strongest version of pancomputationalism. First, all proponents of 
CTC must accept premise (1), since what premise (1) indicates is the core 
thesis of the computational theory of cognition. Second, premise (3) is true. 
Assume that premises (1) and (2) are true. This implies, as Piccinini (2010) 
puts it, that CTC is trivial and vacuous since the human cognitive system is 
already accounted for by pancomputationalism. Searle (1990) states, (if one 
asks whether our cognitive system is a computer) âit would not answer that 
question to be told that the brain [or human cognitive system] is a digital 
computer in the sense in which [the] stomach, liver, heart, solar system, 
and the state of Kansas are all digital computersâ (p. 26). In other words, 
given that everything is a computer that performs every computation, the 
claim that the human cognitive system executes cognitive programs is not 
interesting at all. 
Thus, the remaining option for proponents of CTC is to deny premise 
(2). That is, computational cognitivists should deny the strongest version of 
pancomputationalism. The way I see it, this option is promising despite the 
efforts Searle made in his 1990 paper.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn this section, SeongSoo Park explores the "Triviality Argument" posed by Searle, which challenges the computational theory of cognition (CTC) through pancomputationalism's strongest form. According to this, everything, without exception, can perform any computation.The Triviality Argument is outlined as follows:CTC asserts our cognitive system is a computing system.Pancomputationalism states that everything is a computing system that performs any computation.If both (1) and (2) are true, then CTC is uninformative.Thus, CTC is uninformative.This argument doesn't aim to refute CTC, but rather demonstrates that if pancomputationalism holds true, CTC offers little insight into how our cognitive system works or produces mental phenomena. It essentially implies that if everything can compute, then the claim that human cognitive processes are computational is not exceptional or enlightening.However, Park argues that not all forms of pancomputationalism trivialize CTC. For instance, a weaker version, suggesting that everything is a computing system performing some (not all) computation, would not undermine CTC. CTC could then assert its explanatory power by arguing that our cognitive system executes a unique program not achievable by other computers.Park believes that the only way to counter the Triviality Argument is by rejecting its strongest form of pancomputationalism. He acknowledges that proponents of CTC must accept premise (1) as it's core to their theory. He also considers premise (3) to be true. However, he suggests that proponents of CTC should reject premise (2) - the strongest version of pancomputationalism - as this would alleviate the trivialization of CTC.1 / 1type of computational interpretation to the pattern. Searle did not. He did not even mention how the assignment of 0s and 1s 
should be arranged to implement the WordStar program. The only thing he 
mentioned is that it was a possibility. 
This point would be more evident if we adopt a more fine-grained 
conception of a computer than Searle. Specifically, I distinguish an 
unrealized computer from a realized computer.7 An unrealized computer 
is a physical object (or a collection of physical objects) that merely has the 
possibility to be realized as a computer. This possibility is realized only 
when an appropriate computational interpretation is provided. For instance, 
let us assume that there is a set of cups before us. It seems obvious that 
those cups are not a real computer in themselves. They are just a bunch of 
cups until we assign a specif i c computational interpretation to them. 
If we accept this distinction, it becomes evident that Searleâs treatment 
of pancomputationalism in terms of realized computers is false. 
Pancomputationalism can at most be understood in terms of unrealized

computers. In fact, the view, if properly understood, is only committed to a 
modal claim. At most, pancomputationalism can propound that everything 
is an unrealized computer that might be realized in the future by virtue 
of receiving some specif i c computational interpretation. The wall is not a 
realized computer of the kind we can buy in the store in the sense that it 
has not been assigned any specif i c type of computational interpretation. If 
so, the original Triviality Argument should also be understood in a modal 
way as follows. I will call this modif i ed version of it the Modal Triviality 
Argument. 
1. The computational theory of cognition states that our cognitive system is 
a computing system. 
2. Everything could be a computing system that performs any 
computation. 
3. If (1) and (2), then the computational theory of cognition is not 
informative. 
4. Therefore, the computational theory of cognition is not informative. 
I do not think that this is the result that Searle wanted to argue for. 
Above all, CTC is not refuted by this argument. As we can see in premise 
(2), the Modal Triviality Argument adopts a modal understanding of 
pancomputationalism. According to this understanding, everything is not a 
computer performing any program, but everything could be a computing 
system that performs any computation. However, this modal understanding 
of pancomputationalism is also false, since not all physical objects are 
unrealized computers. This can be argued as follows: 
First, some objects (or collections) might be too simple to be1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn this part of his work, SeongSoo Park brings up the distinction between unrealized and realized computers to counter Searle's understanding of pancomputationalism. An unrealized computer is a physical object (or a collection of physical objects) that merely has the potential to be realized as a computer if a suitable computational interpretation is provided. On the other hand, a realized computer is a concrete computer that has already been assigned a specific computational interpretation.Park argues that Searle's conception of pancomputationalism, which seems to consider everything as realized computers, is incorrect. He posits that pancomputationalism should be understood in terms of unrealized computers. That is, pancomputationalism merely asserts a modal claim: everything is an unrealized computer that could potentially be realized in the future through a specific computational interpretation.Based on this distinction, Park modifies the original Triviality Argument into the "Modal Triviality Argument" as follows:The computational theory of cognition states that our cognitive system is a computing system.Everything could be a computing system that performs any computation.If (1) and (2), then the computational theory of cognition is not informative.Therefore, the computational theory of cognition is not informative.In this argument, pancomputationalism is understood in a modal sense, suggesting that everything could potentially be a computing system, not that everything already is. cognition should be understood in terms of a realized computer.9 However, 
premise (2) of the Restricted Modal Triviality Argument does not relate 
to the notion of a realized computer. Hence, the argument does not refute

Moreover, premise (2) is also false. For example, let us suppose that some 
unrealized computers are only complex enough to perform an addition 
program. If so, they cannot be a computer that implements a complex 
program. That is, they are complex enough to be computers, but they are 
still too simple to implement certain kinds of programs. This implies that 
only some physical objects within type X could qualify as computers 
that can perform cognitive programs. The point is that CTC is not refuted 
by the fact that some objects within type X could potentially be realized 
as computers that run cognitive programs. Indeed, this is the claim that

proponents of CTC want to highlight. According to computational theorists 
of cognition, our cognitive system, in its nature, is not that different from 
sufficiently complex computers. This is why they attempt to explain our 
cognitive system in terms of computation. Therefore, as long as it does not 
trivialize CTC, computational theorists of cognition can embrace the fact 
that some physical objects within type X could potentially be realized as 
computers that can implement cognitive programs. 
7. Conclusion 
This completes my defense of the computational theory of cognition. 
I conclude that the Triviality Argument is unsound because the strongest 
version of pancomputationalism is false. In showing this, I did not directly 
engage with the Chinese Room Argument. I have nothing to say on the 
syntactic conception of computation. However, I have many words to say 
about the Pan Argument. The observer-relativity of computation does not 
guarantee that everything is a realized computer. Any object cannot become 
a computer before a specific computational interpretation is actually 
given to it. Hence, the strongest version of pancomputationalism is false. 
Furthermore, a modal understanding of it is also unconvincing, since not all 
physical objects are unrealized computers. Thus, the most legitimate move 
of pancomputationalism is merely that some physical objects belonging to 
the category of unrealized computer could be a computer that performs any 
program. This peculiar version does not threaten the computational theory 
of cognition. Rather, this is the core claim that computational cognitivists, if 
they adopt the syntactic conception of computation, should embrace.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn this concluding part of his work, SeongSoo Park reiterates his defense of the computational theory of cognition (CTC) against the Triviality Argument. He argues that CTC should be understood in terms of a realized computer, not an unrealized one. Moreover, he says that the Triviality Argument's premise, which suggests that any physical object could be a computer that performs any computation, does not apply to a realized computer, hence it cannot refute CTC.Park further argues that not all unrealized computers have the capacity to perform complex programs due to their simplicity. Therefore, the notion that any physical object can execute any computation is incorrect. Only some physical objects, due to their complexity, can potentially be realized as computers running cognitive programs. He emphasizes that this potential realization doesn't trivialize CTC but actually underscores what computational theorists of cognition aim to highlight - that our cognitive system, in its essence, is not vastly different from sufficiently complex computers.Finally, he concludes that the strongest version of pancomputationalism is false because it posits that everything is a realized computer. He asserts that any object cannot become a computer until a specific computational interpretation is given to it. Furthermore, the modal understanding of pancomputationalism, which suggests that everything could potentially be a computing system, is also not convincing because not all physical objects are unrealized computers. According to him, the most legitimate form of pancomputationalism is that some physical objects belonging to the category of unrealized computers could potentially become computers performing any program. This claim, he suggests, does not threaten the computational theory of cognition, but should actually be embraced by computational cognitivists.1 / 1Pancomputationalism: Theory or metaphor?
Vincent C. MÃ¼ller
In Ruth Hagengruber & Uwe Riss (eds.), Philosophy, computing and information science. Pickering & Chattoo. pp. 213-221 (2014)   Copy   BIBTEX
Abstract
The theory that all processes in the universe are computational is attractive in its promise to provide an understandable theory of everything. I want to suggest here that this pancomputationalism is not sufficiently clear on which problem it is trying to solve, and how. I propose two interpretations of pancomputationalism as a theory: I) the world is a computer and II) the world can be described as a computer. The first implies a thesis of supervenience of the physical over computation and is thus reduced ad absurdum. The second is underdetermined by the world, and thus equally unsuccessful as theory. Finally, I suggest that pancomputationalism as metaphor can be useful. â At the Paderborn workshop in 2008, this paper was presented as a commentary to the relevant paper by Gordana Dodig-Crnkovic " Info-Computationalism and Philosophical Aspects of Research in Information Sciences".1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn this abstract, Vincent C. MÃ¼ller discusses pancomputationalism, the theory that all processes in the universe are computational. While pancomputationalism has a certain allure, promising an understandable theory of everything, MÃ¼ller expresses skepticism about the clarity of its aim and its methodology.MÃ¼ller offers two possible interpretations of pancomputationalism as a theory:I) The world is a computer: This interpretation suggests that everything in the world is a computational process. This could imply that physical processes are essentially computational, leading to a form of supervenience of the physical over computation. MÃ¼ller suggests this interpretation is untenable, and reduces itself to absurdity, as it does not align with our understanding of the physical world.II) The world can be described as a computer: In this interpretation, the world, while not necessarily being a computer, can be described or modeled in terms of computation. This interpretation, however, is argued to be underdetermined by the world, meaning there are not enough constraints in our observations of the world to fully specify this computational model. Thus, this interpretation is also unsuccessful as a theory.Despite his criticisms of pancomputationalism as a theory, MÃ¼ller suggests that pancomputationalism can still be useful as a metaphor. As a metaphor, it could help in conceptualizing complex physical processes and phenomena. This paper was presented as a commentary to a paper by Gordana Dodig-Crnkovic on "Info-Computationalism and Philosophical Aspects of Research in Information Sciences" at a workshop in Paderborn in 2008.1 / 1Representation, Analytic Pragmatism and AI
Raffaela Giovagnoli 
Chapter
981 Accesses

3 Citations

Part of the Studies in Applied Philosophy, Epistemology and Rational Ethics book series (SAPERE,volume 7)

Abstract
Our contribution aims at individuating a valid philosophical strategy for a fruitful confrontation between human and artificial representation. The ground for this theoretical option resides in the necessity to find a solution that overcomes, on the one side, strong AI (i.e. Haugeland) and, on the other side, the view that rules out AI as explanation of human capacities (i.e. Dreyfus). We try to argue for Analytic Pragmatism (AP) as a valid strategy to present arguments for a form of weak AI and to explain a notion of representation common to human and artificial agents1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn this abstract, Raffaela Giovagnoli discusses the relationship between human and artificial representation. Giovagnoli's goal is to find a valid philosophical strategy that allows for productive comparisons and interactions between human and artificial intelligence. This requires a theoretical approach that avoids two extremes:Strong AI, represented by the work of philosopher John Haugeland, which proposes that artificial intelligence (AI) can replicate or surpass human intelligence.The view articulated by philosopher Hubert Dreyfus that categorically rejects AI as an explanation for human capacities.Giovagnoli proposes Analytic Pragmatism (AP) as a potential solution. AP combines the clarity and precision of analytic philosophy with the practical and action-oriented focus of pragmatism. She argues that AP is a valid strategy for advocating for a form of weak AI, which holds that AI can simulate certain aspects of human intelligence but does not replicate the totality of human cognition.Using AP, Giovagnoli aims to explain a notion of representation that can be applicable to both human and artificial agents. Representation here refers to the ability of both humans and AI systems to create mental or digital models of the world that guide their actions and decision-making. By adopting the AP strategy, Giovagnoli hopes to find a middle ground that recognizes the potential of AI without overstating its capabilities or underestimating the complexity of human cognition.1 / 1Connections between the articles1 / 2ChatGPTChatGPTThe two articles "The Problem of Pancomputationalism: Focusing on Three Related Arguments" by Seongsoo Park and "Representation, Analytic Pragmatism and AI" by Raffaela Giovagnoli, both delve into philosophical aspects of artificial intelligence (AI), specifically in relation to human cognition and the nature of computation.Pancomputationalism vs. Info-Computationalism: Pancomputationalism, as discussed by Park, suggests that everything in the universe is a computational process. It challenges the computational theory of cognition (CTC), which posits that our cognitive system works like a computational system, by arguing that if everything is computational, then CTC becomes trivial. Vincent C. MÃ¼ller's "Pancomputationalism: Theory or metaphor?" discussed by Park, provides further context, questioning the validity of pancomputationalism as a comprehensive theory. On the other hand, Dodig-Crnkovic's info-computationalism, which is mentioned in MÃ¼ller's paper, posits that computation combined with information forms the fabric of reality, a position that seeks to reconcile universal computation with the uniqueness of cognitive processes.Understanding of AI and Human cognition: Giovagnoli's approach in "Representation, Analytic Pragmatism and AI" also deals with the understanding of AI and human cognition. She proposes analytic pragmatism as a way to avoid extremes (i.e., strong AI and AI denial), aiming for a weak AI position that recognizes AI as simulating aspects of human intelligence but not fully replicating it.Representation: Both Park and Giovagnoli delve into the concept of representation in cognition. Park's arguments revolve around the idea that computational interpretation, essentially a form of representation, is necessary for something to be considered a computing system. Similarly, Giovagnoli's focus is on finding a common ground between human and artificial representation.In summary, both articles grapple with the philosophical implications of viewing cognition and the universe through a computational lens. They offer different perspectives on how AI and human cognition relate, how to conceptualize the universe's computational nature, and how representations play a crucial role in these discussions.1 / 1InfoâComputationalism and Philosophical Aspects of Research

This perspective is not confined to natural sciences but extends to all forms of human knowledge, embracing the global interconnectedness facilitated by information technology.The implications for artificial intelligence (AI) and cognitive science are profound. It suggests that living organisms can be modeled as info-computational agents, with their biological processes interpreted as computation on information. Moreover, it indicates that technology can, in principle, simulate the essential abilities of living organisms to process and structure information.ICON also provides a framework for understanding cognitive functions such as perception, thinking, reasoning, will, feelings, and memory, offering a computational naturalist model of cognition.Importantly, DodigâCrnkovic highlights the bidirectional relationship between philosophical thinking and scientific findings. Philosophy guides how we interpret and understand scientific results, while scientific discoveries, in turn, inspire and inform philosophical speculation. This dynamic exchange enriches both domains, pushing the boundaries of our knowledge and understanding.1 / 11. Science and Philosophy  
âOur best efforts are directed at finding out why things are as they are or why the

In doing so, it seeks to understand how the world is put together and operates.Science, for its part, is driven by the pursuit of knowledge, both propositional (expressed in declarative sentences or indicative propositions) and non-propositional. Often, knowledge is equated with propositional knowledge, and science is seen as the pursuit of propositional truth about the world. However, DodigâCrnkovic emphasizes that science can also be seen as a goal-driven human activity aimed at creating models that allow us to predict, correlate, and structure information about the world.She then discusses the evolution of the concept of natural law from deterministic order based on the principle of causality to the recognition of indeterminism in quantum physics and the unpredictability even within deterministic systems. She emphasizes that what is crucial to scientific knowledge is not its certainty (as concepts like time, space, mass, and trajectory have been successively revised), but the rationality of its approaches, presuppositions, and aims.With the advent of powerful computational technologies, the complexity of real-world problems has come into focus, challenging the traditional view of a static, symmetrical, steady-state order. Instead, a dynamic picture emerges, where everything changes and order is seen as a pattern over pulsating underlying physical processes. Simple basic rules can lead to the evolution of complex systems, a perspective that complements info-computationalism and the pancomputationalist view of the world.1 / 13. 7. Answering some Usual Kinds of Criticism of InfoâComputationalism  
Info-Computationalist view may be interpreted as a claim that the whole of the 
world is ânothing but a (computational) machineâ and that we humans are essen- 
tially machines with no free will or feelings. That is obviously not the case. The 
view that the universe is an info-computational phenomenon means that the un- 
iverse as it is may be understood and modeled in info-computational language. 
Feelings, qualia and other mental phenomena are emergent properties of the 
physical world which is info-computational.   
The role of different paradigms in our understanding of the universe can be ana- 
lyzed on historical examples. In the past, several major paradigm shifts occurred: 
from mytho-poetic to mechanistic to the emerging computationalist understand- 
ing of the universe. Consequently, we can ask the same question about mytho- 
poetic and mechanist universe: Was that understanding of the universe true? Was 
it real or merely metaphoric? For the mytho-poetic universe, the answer is simple 
â it was a metaphor. Even though mechanicism was primarily the outlook at the 
inanimate matter, and mechanistic approaches to robotics (mechanical quasi- 
humans) did not work for any other purpose but the entertainment, mechanistic 
worldview nevertheless helped us to learn a lot about the universe, in the first 
place inanimate but even many basic facts about living world (such as that there is 
no Ãlan vital but the same physical mechanistic laws govern the whole of the 
physical universe, living as well as non-living).   
The parallel development goes on in the course of computationalism now again. 
We will learn about informational and computational resources and capabilities, 
14/16 
of the universe and we will develop even more powerful ways of learning, via 
intelligent systems we will successively improve.   
Knowing that biological organisms (including humans) are information- 
processing âmachinesâ does not make them less fascinating. In the same way as 
knowing that all of us are made of atoms does not mean that we do not have free 
will, imagination and real feelings. Understanding fundamental level processes 
does not make music, arts and philosophy obsolete.   
Info-computationalism helps us both by supplying the tools for knowledge and 
artifact production and even tools for understanding of the natural phenomena 
and artifacts on many different levels. That is also why philosophy is coming 
back to sciences based on info-computational knowledge. A holistic, high level of 
abstraction view is necessary as a self-reflective process of knowledge.

It proposes that our minds function similarly to a computer, processing information and making decisions based on certain rules and algorithms.

Pancomputationalism: We discussed this philosophical position which holds that every part of the universe is a computational system or can be modeled as such.

The Triviality Argument: We explored this argument which asserts that if everything is a computing system that performs any computation (as per pancomputationalism), then the Computational Theory of Cognition becomes uninformative and trivial.

Searle's Objections to Pancomputationalism: We discussed philosopher John Searle's criticism of pancomputationalism and his arguments against it, including his critique that pancomputationalism does not clarify how computation is assigned or understood within a system.

Representation in AI and Humans: We examined the topic of representation within both human cognition and artificial intelligence. We discussed how the theory of Analytic Pragmatism could serve as a framework for understanding and comparing representation in both humans and AI systems.

Strong AI vs. Weak AI: We touched upon the debate between strong AI (the belief that AI can fully replicate human intelligence) and weak AI (the belief that AI can only simulate certain aspects of human cognition).
1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTInfo-Computationalism, as discussed earlier, has strong connections to all these topics:The Computational Theory of Cognition (CTC): Info-Computationalism and CTC both view cognitive processes as computational in nature. Both theories propose that cognition, whether human or artificial, involves the processing of information, similar to how a computer operates.Pancomputationalism: Info-Computationalism can be seen as a variant of pancomputationalism in that it proposes the universe as an informational-computational system. However, Info-Computationalism emphasizes the transformation of data into information and knowledge, rather than simply positing everything as a computational system.The Triviality Argument: This argument posits that if everything is a computational system (as per pancomputationalism), theories like CTC or Info-Computationalism become trivial. However, the emphasis on a hierarchy of organization from data to information to knowledge in Info-Computationalism counters this triviality, providing a framework for understanding the complexity of such systems.Searle's Objections to Pancomputationalism: Info-Computationalism may offer a response to Searle's objections by providing a more structured and less broad view of the universe as an informational-computational system. It distinguishes between data, information, and knowledge, and emphasizes the necessity of work to transform one into the other.Representation in AI and Humans: In Info-Computationalism, the representation of knowledge can be understood as the organization of information within a system. It could provide a philosophical grounding for understanding representation in both humans and AI.Strong AI vs. Weak AI: Info-Computationalism doesn't necessarily take a side in this debate. However, it provides a theoretical framework in which the progression from Weak AI (which could be likened to handling 'data') to Strong AI (which would involve the manipulation and understanding of 'knowledge') could be explored and understood.1 / 1It seems that pancomputationalism is often called Digital physics, or even Mathematical universe hypothesis.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTYes, pancomputationalism does share a conceptual affinity with concepts such as digital physics and the mathematical universe hypothesis. These ideas posit a fundamentally computational or mathematical substrate to reality.Digital Physics: This is the notion that the entire history of our universe is computable and can be thought of as the evolution of a cellular automaton or similar digital computing device. It proposes that reality is essentially informational, and physics, at its core, can be described entirely in terms of information theory.Mathematical Universe Hypothesis (MUH): This is a more radical proposition put forth by physicist Max Tegmark. It suggests that the universe is not merely described by mathematics, but that it is a mathematical structure in its entirety. Every object, including you and me, is an abstract mathematical object.While they all have similarities, it's crucial to distinguish these concepts. Pancomputationalism generally asserts that everything can be modeled as a computation. In contrast, digital physics goes a step further to suggest that the universe is fundamentally digital and informational. The MUH takes an even more extreme position, positing the universe itself as a purely mathematical structure. Each of these perspectives offers a unique lens through which to understand reality, and each comes with its own set of philosophical implications and debates.1 / 1Ok any other debate theories?1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTYes, there are several other theories and debates within the philosophy of mind and cognitive science that relate to the computational understanding of the world and consciousness. Here are a few:Functionalism: This is the theory that mental states are defined by their functional roles rather than their intrinsic properties. It argues that what makes something a mental state is not its internal constitution but its role in the overall psychological system.Connectionism: This theory emphasizes the idea that human cognition and learning are best understood in terms of network structures (like neural networks) and their interrelated properties. It is often contrasted with computational approaches that rely on symbolic reasoning.Embodied Cognition: This is the idea that cognitive processes are deeply rooted in the body's interactions with the world. It challenges the computational model of cognition that views the mind as a disembodied system.Extended Mind Thesis: This concept suggests that the tools and technologies we interact with can become part of our cognitive process, effectively extending our mind into the world.Chinese Room Argument: Proposed by John Searle, this thought experiment is designed to argue against the notion of "strong AI," or the idea that machines can truly understand and have consciousness.Turing Test and its criticisms: Proposed by Alan Turing, the Turing Test is a measure of a machine's ability to exhibit intelligent behavior equivalent to, or indistinguishable from, human behavior. There have been many criticisms of this test and what it actually demonstrates.Physicalism vs Dualism: This is a classic debate about the nature of consciousness. Physicalism asserts that all mental states are physical states, while Dualism holds that mental states are distinct and separate from physical states.Phenomenal Consciousness and Access Consciousness: Proposed by Ned Block, this is a distinction between two types of consciousness. Abstract
The Free Energy Principle (FEP) states that under suitable conditions of weak coupling, random dynamical systems with sufficient degrees of freedom will behave so as to minimize an upper bound, formalized as a variational free energy, on surprisal (a.k.a., self-information). This upper bound can be read as a Bayesian prediction error. Equivalently, its negative is a lower bound on Bayesian model evidence (a.k.a., marginal likelihood). In short, certain random dynamical systems evince a kind of self-evidencing. Here, we reformulate the FEP in the formal setting of spacetime-background free, scale-free quantum information theory. We show how generic quantum systems can be regarded as observers, which with the standard freedom of choice assumption become agents capable of assigning semantics to observational outcomes. We show how such agents minimize Bayesian prediction error in environments characterized by uncertainty, insufficient learning, and quantum contextuality. We show that in its quantum-theoretic formulation, the FEP is asymptotically equivalent to the Principle of Unitarity. Based on these results, we suggest that biological systems employ quantum coherence as a computational resource and â implicitly â as a communication resource. We summarize a number of problems for future research, particularly involving the resources required for classical communication and for detecting and responding to quantum context switches.

Introduction
Since its introduction as a theory of brain function (Friston, 2005, 2010; Friston et al., 2006; Friston and Stephan, 2007), the variational Free Energy Principle (FEP) has been extended into an explanatory framework for living systems at all scales (Friston, 2013; Friston et al., 2017a; Ramstead et al., 2018, 2019; Kuchling et al., 2020), and shown to characterize, in its most general form, all random dynamical systems that remain measurable, and hence identifiable as persistent and separable entities, over macroscopic times (Friston, 2019). To summarize, it is shown in (Friston, 2019) that any system that has a non-equilibrium steady state (NESS) solution to its density dynamics i) possesses an internal dynamics that is conditionally independent of the dynamics of its environment, and ii) will continuously âself-evidenceâ by returning its state to (the vicinity of) its NESS. The FEP is the statement that any measurable, i.e. bounded and macroscopically persistent, system will behave so as to satisfy these requirements. In particular, the classical FEP as developed in (Friston, 2019) considers the joint environment-agent system as a random dynamical system that possesses an attracting set. By placing particular constraints on the coupling among systemic states (e.g., with sparse coupling in flow operators or stochastic differential equations), one can partition the joint state space into external, internal and Markov blanket (MB; see (Clark, 2017) for an informal review) states. In turn, the blanket states are partitioned into sensory states that mediate the influence of external states on internal states and active states that mediate the influence of internal states on external states. Crucially, this particular partition imposes conditional independence1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe Free Energy Principle (FEP) for quantum systems is an extension of the original FEP as applied in neuroscience, but extended to quantum systems. FEP was first developed by Karl Friston as a framework to explain the brain's function, and has since been extended to explain the behaviors of all living systems.In simple terms, FEP suggests that our brains (and, by extension, all adaptive living systems) are constantly predicting the state of the environment and then minimizing the difference between the prediction and the actual sensory input. This is formalized as a variational free energy, which acts as an upper bound on the prediction error (surprisal or self-information). When the free energy is minimized, the system is in a state of least surprise, indicating that its model of the world is accurate.When applied to quantum systems, the Free Energy Principle suggests that these systems also behave in a way to minimize Bayesian prediction error in environments characterized by uncertainty, insufficient learning, and quantum contextuality.Quantum contextuality refers to the idea that the outcome of a measurement on a quantum system can depend on the specific context in which the measurement is made (such as the entire set of measurements that are performed together). So in this setting, quantum systems are considered to behave as if they were agents, assigning semantics to observational outcomes and minimizing prediction errors in environments with these specific characteristics.Furthermore, the paper suggests that the quantum version of FEP is asymptotically equivalent to the Principle of Unitarity, a key principle in quantum mechanics that states that the evolution of a quantum system is deterministic and reversible.Finally, the paper makes an intriguing suggestion that biological systems may be using quantum coherence as a computational and communication resource, and proposes a number of areas for future research.In essence, the Free Energy Principle for quantum systems is suggesting a form of quantum "self-evidencing", whereby the system continuously acts to maintain a model of the world that minimizes prediction error.1 / 1Self-organization, the FEP tells us, is not a rare, special case, but a ubiquitous feature of physical systems with sufficient dynamical stability to be called âthings.â All âthings,â in particular, self-organize MBs comprising âsensoryâ states that encode incoming information and thus mediate the influence of external states on internal states, and âactiveâ states that encode outgoing information and thus mediate the influence of internal states on external states. This partitioning of âthingsâ into internal and MB states means that every âthingâ can be construed as a certain kind of âparticleâ â a particle that is in open exchange with external states via its MB. In short, the MB of any such âparticleâ underwrites conditional independence between its internal states and the external states of its environment by localizing and thereby restricting information exchange; hence, the MB can be viewed as separating internal from external states, while mediating their exchange.

As noted in (Friston, 2019), generalizing the FEP to characterize the behavior of all âthingsâ substantially weakens the traditional distinction between âcognitionâ and merely âphysicalâ dynamics, and hence weakens the even deeper, pretheoretical (Scholl and Tremoulet, 2000) distinction between âagentsâ and mere âobjectsâ (Fields et al., 2021a). Treating physical interaction as information exchange â âobservationâ of the environment followed by âactionâ upon it â redescribes the âmechanicalâ process of returning to the NESS â as a random global (a.k.a., pullback) attractor â in terms of inference. This can be read as âactive inferenceâ (Friston, 2010, 2013, 2019; Friston et al., 2017a) in which the existential integrity of the MB, and hence of the âselfâ â âworldâ distinction (Levin, 2020), can be maintained in the face of environmental fluctuations by changing internal states (via sensory states: c.f., perception) or changing external states (via active states: c.f., action). It refocuses the discussion, in other words, from abstract trajectories (or flows) in state space to the MB itself as a concrete locus of âidentityâ in the form of persistent measurability, and hence of âself-evidencingâ via active inference to maintain that identity.

It is worth emphasizing that the FEP is a theory of what can be termed, in the language of cognitive or computer science (Pylyshyn, 1984), the functional architectures of physical systems. It describes the information processing functions that physical systems execute, but is agnostic about their implementation. As first established by Church (1936) and Turing (1936), information processing functions are implementation-independent in principle; provided the generic capabilities of a Turing machine (or of AND and NOT gates), choice of implementation determines resource requirements including time, space and free energy, but does not affect what can be computed. Implementation independence â also sometimes termed âmultiple realizabilityâ (Fodor, 1968, Putnam) â underlies the black box methodology of classical cybernetics (Ashby, 1956), in particular Moore's theorem (Moore, 1956) showing that the internal processes of a black box cannot be determined by finite observation. Indeed an MB can be viewed as implementing the input/output (I/O) interface of a black box; in more general terms, it can be viewed as implementing the application programming interface (API) of a modularized computing system (Booch et al., 2007). An MB can, therefore, be viewed as encapsulating, in the sense of (Pylyshyn, 1984) or of computer science generally, information about implementation. In the language of (Laughlin and Pines, 2000), an MB implements a âquantum protectorateâ of the system it encloses.

The idea that all physical systems, including the environment at large, can be considered âobserversâ that also act on their surroundings to âprepareâ them for subsequent observations has become commonplace in quantum theory, largely replacing the âwave-function collapseâ postulate of traditional quantum mechanics (Von Neumann, 1955) with interaction-induced decoherence (i.e., dissipation of quantum coherence) as the generator of classical information (OmnÃ¨s, 1992; Zurek, 1998; Zurek, 2003; Schlosshauer, 2003; Schlosshauer, 2007).1 Indeed while quantum theory was originally developed â and is still widely regarded â as a theory specifically applicable at the atomic scale and below, since the pioneering work of Wheeler (1983), Feynman (1982), and Deutsch (1985), it has, over the past few decades, been reformulated as a scale-free information theory (Nielsen and Chuang, 2000; Hardy, 2001; Fuchs, 2003; Brassard, 2005; Chiribella et al., 2011;1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe text you've shared describes the generalization of the Free Energy Principle (FEP) to all physical systems, not just cognitive or biological ones. Essentially, the FEP applies to any system that maintains its existence over time in a changing environment. This wide applicability blurs the traditional distinction between cognition and mere physical dynamics, or between agents and mere objects.The Free Energy Principle, in this broader context, can be understood as a kind of 'active inference', where a system maintains its integrity in the face of environmental fluctuations. The Sheaf-Theoretic Structure Of Non-Locality and Contextuality
Samson Abramsky, Adam Brandenburger
We use the mathematical language of sheaf theory to give a unified treatment of non-locality and contextuality, in a setting which generalizes the familiar probability tables used in non-locality theory to arbitrary measurement covers; this includes Kochen-Specker configurations and more. We show that contextuality, and non-locality as a special case, correspond exactly to obstructions to the existence of global sections. We describe a linear algebraic approach to computing these obstructions, which allows a systematic treatment of arguments for non-locality and contextuality. We distinguish a proper hierarchy of strengths of no-go theorems, and show that three leading examples --- due to Bell, Hardy, and Greenberger, Horne and Zeilinger, respectively --- occupy successively higher levels of this hierarchy. A general correspondence is shown between the existence of local hidden-variable realizations using negative probabilities, and no-signalling; this is based on a result showing that the linear subspaces generated by the non-contextual and no-signalling models, over an arbitrary measurement cover, coincide. Maximal non-locality is generalized to maximal contextuality, and characterized in purely qualitative terms, as the non-existence of global sections in the support. A general setting is developed for Kochen-Specker type results, as generic, model-independent proofs of maximal contextuality, and a new combinatorial condition is given, which generalizes the `parity proofs' commonly found in the literature. We also show how our abstract setting can be represented in quantum mechanics. This leads to a strengthening of the usual no-signalling theorem, which shows that quantum mechanics obeys no-signalling for arbitrary families of commuting observables, not just those represented on different factors of a tensor product.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe paper you mentioned, "The Sheaf-Theoretic Structure Of Non-Locality and Contextuality," discusses how sheaf theoryâa branch of mathematicsâcan provide a framework for understanding phenomena such as non-locality and contextuality in quantum physics. Non-locality refers to the phenomenon in quantum mechanics where changes to a particle can instantaneously affect another particle, regardless of the distance between them (e.g., entangled particles). Conclusion
We have seen here how the FEP provides a generic model of MCAs that applies equally to natural and engineered systems and equally to short and long timescales. Indeed the FEP erases the distinction between natural and engineered systems. Because the FEP characterizes the environment of any system of interest as an agent, the environment can always be regarded as âtrainingâ or âengineeringâ the system. The ubiquitous role of the environment in providing the parts required for replication1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe provided text explores some aspects of the Free Energy Principle (FEP) and its implications.Physical systems and Markov blanket (MB): The FEP applies to persistent physical systems with a well-defined state space. If a system's internal states are conditionally independent of its environment's states, the system possesses a Markov blanket. This blanket, which is essentially a boundary, conditions the dependence of internal states on external environmental states.Informational symmetry of physical interactions: The principle sees physical interactions as symmetric in terms of information exchange. A cycle of interaction between a system (S) and its environment (E) involves encoding information by E, perceived by S through its sensory states, which then mediates the effect of E on S's internal states. This symmetric view applies equally to any system and its environment.Environment as an active agent: Rather than considering environmental processes as external to a system, the FEP suggests the environment is an active agent that interacts with and influences the system. This viewpoint removes the distinction between natural and engineered systems, positing that the environment always plays a role in training or engineering a system.Implications for understanding cognition and agency: The FEP broadens the definition of 'cognition' and 'agency', beyond traditional conceptions which are often tied to biological organisms or complex machines. By considering all persistent systems as possessing a form of agency mediated by their Markov blanket, we can think about cognition in terms of information processing and adaptive response to the environment. This extends to everything from cells, to human brains, to artificial intelligence systems, and potentially even to non-living systems like rivers or weather patterns.The FEP is thus a theoretical framework for understanding how any systemâbe it biological, physical, or artificialâinteracts with its environment. It suggests a form of universal 'cognition' or 'agency' that all systems exhibit, providing a potential common ground for understanding diverse phenomena in the natural world.1 / 1Sorry, I forgot to mention - that was from this article: Elsevier

Regulative development as a model for origin of life and artificial life studies
Author links open overlay panelChris Fields a, Michael Levin a b

Abstract
Using the formal framework of the Free Energy Principle, we show how generic thermodynamic requirements on bidirectional information exchange between a system and its environment can generate complexity. This leads to the emergence of hierarchical computational architectures in systems that operate sufficiently far from thermal equilibrium. In this setting, the environment of any system increases its ability to predict system behavior by âengineeringâ the system towards increased morphological complexity and hence larger-scale, more macroscopic behaviors. When seen in this light, regulative development becomes an environmentally-driven process in which âpartsâ are assembled to produce a system with predictable behavior. We suggest on this basis that life is thermodynamically favorable and that, when designing artificial living systems, human engineers are acting like a generic âenvironmentâ.

Introduction
Living systems are hierarchical arrangements of components that, critically, exhibit a multiscale competency architecture (MCA; Levin, 2022; Clawson and Levin, 2022). In an MCA, components at each scale are competent to perform the functions appropriate to that scale without explicit top-down instructions. Human cells, for example, do not have to be told how to divide by the brain, or by any larger-scale system; they are competent to divide on their own. At any scale j in an MCA, the behaviors of the components at the lower scale j â 1 provide enabling mechanisms for j-appropriate behaviors, while the behaviors of the components at the higher scale j + 1 impose boundary conditions. As the boundary conditions effectively constrain the possibility space at scale j, state changes at scale j + 1 can modulate or deform the possibility space at scale j, providing a source of top-down novelty. Cell division, for example, is enabled by gene expression within the cell, and constrained by signaling from, or transmitted via, the surrounding multicellular tissue. Top-down âinstructionsâ from the surrounding, or even distal (McMillan et al., 2021), tissue can increase or decrease the rate of cell division, e.g. during wound healing; other examples include the modulation of cancer cell properties by the microenvironment (Bissell et al., 2002; Ingber, 2008; Bizzarri and Cucina, 2014), control of stem cell fate by large-scale axial patterning cues during regeneration (Durant et al., 2017), and local remodeling of a structure based on its larger anatomical context (Farinella-Ferruzza, 1956)1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe excerpt you provided is from a Biosystems journal article titled "Regulative development as a model for origin of life and artificial life studies" by Chris Fields and Michael Levin.This study uses the Free Energy Principle (FEP) to argue that the thermodynamic requirements for bidirectional information exchange between a system and its environment can spur the creation of complexity, leading to the formation of hierarchical computational architectures in systems operating sufficiently far from thermal equilibrium. In this context, the environment is seen as a player in the system's morphological complexity, nudging it towards more extensive, macroscopic behaviors.A concept at the heart of this work is the Multi-scale Competency Architecture (MCA), which is an integral part of living systems. In an MCA, each component level is competent to perform functions pertinent to that scale without needing explicit top-down instructions. The article's authors argue that this independence at each scale, combined with influence from the environment, can shape and drive complex behaviors in both biological and artificial systems.This perspective reshapes our understanding of life and living systems, suggesting that life's origin and existence could be thermodynamically favorable, and that the environment plays a significant role in engineering life's complexity. The authors extend this idea to the design of artificial life systems, proposing that human engineers, in essence, are acting like a generic "environment" when creating such systems.In other words, this approach to understanding living systems is rooted in the principles of thermodynamics and information theory, presenting life and cognition as fundamentally intertwined with the environment and the broader thermodynamic context. This provides a new lens through which to consider questions about the origins of life, the nature of cognition, and the design of artificial life.1 / 1By providing a scale-free, effectively thermodynamic definition of competence, the FEP enables a scale-free biology that treats evolution and development as manifestations (albeit at different levels of organization) of a single process â VFE minimization (Fields and Levin, 2020a, 2020b). It is natural to extend this conception of biology to encompass biologically-relevant prebiotic, abiotic, or exobiotic processes, including those invoked in origin of life and artificial life scenarios. To investigate the definition of âcompetenceâ provided by the FEP in this larger context, we will focus, in particular, on two kinds of biological self-organization:
Regulative development: Using information available from their surrounding environment(s), some number of cells self-organize into a multicellular organism. In both embryonic development and regeneration, such regulated self-organization enables multicellular systems to reach ânormalâ target morphologies despite significant perturbations or altered starting conditions (Birnbaum and Alvarado, 2008; Levin, 2011; Vandenberg et al., 2012; Lobo et al., 2014; Pezzulo and Levin, 2016; Pinet and McLaughlin, 2019; Fields and Levin, 2020b). Such processes are also employed by heterogeneous, facultative multicellular systems like microbial mats.

Ab initio self organization: Some number of molecules self-organize into a cell. The result could also be a self-sustaining proto-cell as in some origin-of-life models.


Regulative development has been studied for over two centuries, and many model systems are now understood at a substantial level of detail including, in several cases, cell-specific gene expression profiles (Tintori et al., 2016; Farnsworth et al., 2020; Wang et al., 2022). Examples of ab initio self-organization, on the other hand, remain hypothetical. While a variety of models have been proposed, mostly in an origin-of-life context, none have been fully implemented experimentally and there is little consensus even about biological plausibility. The goal of any scale-free explanation is to tell exactly the same kind of story about these two examples. Constructing such an explanation by requiring examples of ab initio self-organization to be both formal and mechanistic analogs of regulative development raises a number of issues, mainly concerning the structure and role of the âenvironmentâ in ab initio self-organization, that have been relatively neglected by previous approaches.

In particular, we will see that treating ab initio self-organization as an analog of regulative development challenges two deeply-entrenched ideas. First, it questions the near-universal assumption that any ab initio model must result in a self-replicating system. Evolutionary models from Darwin onwards have strongly coupled variation with inheritance via either meiotic or mitotic cell division (Monod, 1972; SzathmÃ¡ry and Maynard Smith, 1995; Michod, 1999). Natural selection acts on variants, and hence depends on Darwinian models of reproduction. Such models suggest ab initio processes that involve self-replicating molecules immediately. We will suggest that models in which the needed âpartsâ are generated by environmental processes that are at most weakly coupled to the systems of interest are also worth consideration. Variation generated by weakly coupled processes is consistent with evolution at the global scale, but does not depend on natural selection at any single local scale; it thus provides a âNon-Darwinianâ source of order. Such weak coupling is exemplified by situations involving self-organizing systems that include engineered and manufactured components; we suggest that weak-coupling models may be realistic in other settings as well.

Second, treating ab initio self-organization as an analog of regulative development challenges the very idea of self-organization. In its purest form, the idea of self-organization suggests that the information needed for organization is present in the self. This immediately raises the essentially unanswerable question of how this critical information got there to begin with. Directly relating ab initio self-organization to regulative development forces us to ask, at each step of the process, what systems count as âselves,â how the environment of each âselfâ is defined, and how, in each case, the exchange of information between âselfâ and environment is implemented (Levin, 2021).1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTIn this part of the paper, the authors present an approach to understanding biological organization and complexity that treats evolution and development as manifestations of a single process under the Free Energy Principle (FEP) - the minimization of Variational Free Energy (VFE). This approach extends beyond the biological domain to encompass prebiotic, abiotic, and exobiotic processes, which are particularly relevant for theories of the origin of life and artificial life.The authors highlight two forms of biological self-organization:Regulative development: Cells use information from their surroundings to self-organize into a multicellular organism. This process enables multicellular systems to reach normal target morphologies despite significant disturbances or altered starting conditions.Ab initio self-organization: Molecules self-organize to form a cell or a self-sustaining proto-cell, a concept often invoked in origin-of-life models.The authors note that while regulative development is well studied, instances of ab initio self-organization remain largely hypothetical.Taking ab initio self-organization as analogous to regulative development, they challenge two deep-seated ideas:The assumption that any ab initio model must result in a self-replicating system. Instead, the authors suggest considering models where the necessary "parts" are generated by environmental processes that are at most weakly coupled to the systems of interest.The very notion of self-organization. The authors propose that the concept of self-organization, in its pure form, where information needed for organization is present within the system itself, raises the question of how such information originated. They recommend analyzing how the exchange of information between the "self" and the environment occurs at each stage of the process.These discussions provide a novel perspective on the emergence of complex systems and life itself, suggesting that the environment plays a more active role in this process than traditionally thought. They also underscore the importance of considering interactions at various scales, from individual cells to whole organisms, and the dynamic, ongoing exchanges of information that occur between these entities and their environments.1 / 1Our analysis suggests two broad conclusions:
1.
Reproduction via cell division is an efficiency hack â a cheap heuristic â which evolution âfroze inâ by selecting strong self/other recognition systems starting in bacteria. Decoupling replication from self-organization generalizes the systems of interest to developmental studies toward âegalitarianâ (Strassmann and Queller, 2010) assemblages of unrelated, or in the evolved case, only distantly related, components that âjust happenâ to work well together. This allows simpler origin of life stories. It acknowledges the possibility of kinematic replication in biological systems, as observed experimentally in âxenobotsâ constructed from dissociated Xenopus laevis skin cells (Kriegman et al., 2021). It suggests that symbiotic systems such as microbial mats are âcanonicalâ to at least the same extent as obligately sexual multicellulars. The focus of both ab initio and developmental studies becomes, in this case, how components interact once they are placed in mutual proximity, independently of their origins.

2.
âSelf-organizationâ is always environment-dependent, so we can view it as at least in part environment-directed: some of the instructive information is initially in the environment, not in the self that assembles. From the perspective of physical interaction (Fields and MarcianÃ², 2020; Fields, Glazebrook and MarcianÃ², 2021) or of the FEP (Friston, 2019; Ramstead et al., 2022; Fields et al., 2022), this is obvious. It is also obvious in many origin of life models, in microbial mat assembly, and in embryogenesis, even in âEuropean planâ organisms like C. elegans (BarriÃ¨re and Bertrand, 2020). It is, nonetheless, often neglected or de-emphasized, particularly in theoretical work. As discussed in Fields et al. (2022), the FEP applies to the environment of any system of interest, rendering it an uncertainty-minimizing agent as discussed in Â§4 below. The environment-dependence of self-organization is a consequence of the environment acting as a Bayesian agent.


In what follows, we first review in Â§2 both the classical development of the FEP and its quantum information-theoretic generalization. As the latter provides the most convenient formalism for describing the system-environment interaction, we adopt it in what follows. We then discuss in Â§3 a fundamental symmetry of the FEP that is often neglected: the FEP requires the environment of any system to also be a VFE-minimizing agent. While any system-environment interaction is informationally symmetric â equal quantities of information flow in both directions â what the two parties do with the information they receive may be radically different. Symmetric information flows, in other words, are consistent with âcognitive light conesâ (Levin, 2021, 2022) of different widths and depths, and hence different active inference capabilities, on the two sides of the system-environment boundary. We turn in Â§4 to an explicit comparison between regulative development and ab initio self-organization, focusing first on characterizing the environments of each active component as information sources, and hence as themselves active agents, at each step in the process. We investigate, in particular, how both regulative development and ab initio self-organization decrease the VFE detected by the environment, making them thermodynamically-driven processes under the FEP. The environment of any system, in other words, can be expected to act so as to increase that system's complexity, with the likely outcome being a complexity-increasing âarms raceâ between the system and its environment if both have sufficient computational resources. We conclude by outlining a âmix and matchâ experimental strategy suggested by these results in Â§5.1 / 1ChatGPTChatGPTThe authors draw two primary conclusions from their analysis:The process of reproduction through cell division, which is a common feature in life as we know it, is seen as an "efficiency hack" that evolution selected. Neurons as hierarchies of quantum reference frames
Author links open overlay panelChris Fields a, James F. Glazebrook b c, Michael Levin d
Show more

Abstract
Conceptual and mathematical models of neurons have lagged behind empirical understanding for decades. Here we extend previous work in modeling biological systems with fully scale-independent quantum information-theoretic tools to develop a uniform, scalable representation of synapses, dendritic and axonal processes, neurons, and local networks of neurons. In this representation, hierarchies of quantum reference frames act as hierarchical active-inference systems. The resulting model enables specific predictions of correlations between synaptic activity, dendritic remodeling, and trophic reward. We summarize how the model may be generalized to nonneural cells and tissues in developmental and regenerative contexts.

Introduction
Neurons are canonical biological information processors. Theoretical and, particularly, conceptual models of neural information processing, however, lag increasingly far behind our developing empirical understanding of neurons as electrically-excitable cells. Experimental work over the past two decades has, for example, firmly established that dendrites undergo activity-dependent remodeling (Butz et al., 2009, Carulli et al., 2011, Hogan et al., 2020), particularly alterations of spine location, density, and function (Runge et al., 2020), even in adult humans. This ontogenic process is functionally analogous to the evolution of structural and positional diversity of dendrites as they have adapted to a spectrum of functional roles (Wittenberg and Wang, 2016), e.g. the implementation of deep learning via synaptic plasticity (Guerguiev et al., 2017, Sardi et al., 2018). Neurons are not, therefore, static structures, but rather can be regarded as undergoing continuous development throughout the life cycle. This dynamic process has significant consequences for both neuron-level and organism-level function. For example, in organisms (such as caterpillars transitioning into butterflies or moths) that exhibit drastic remodeling and reconstruction of their brains, some of their learned memories remain and survive the process (Blackiston et al., 2015). In other contexts, memories can be imprinted on newly regenerating brains from other tissues (Shomrat and Levin, 2013, McConnell, 1967), underscoring the plasticity of large-scale neural structure and of the information stored within it. These effects of remodeling are not just an issue for so-called lower animals, as applications in regenerative medicine are likely to soon produce human patients in whom some portion of the brain has been replaced by the progeny of naÃ¯ve stem cells to treat degenerative disease or brain damage.1 / 2ChatGPTChatGPTThe authors present a novel approach to modeling neurons using quantum information-theoretic tools. This approach aims to create a uniform, scalable representation of synapses, dendritic and axonal processes, neurons, and local networks of neurons, in which hierarchies of quantum reference frames function as hierarchical active-inference systems. The model can make specific predictions about correlations between synaptic activity, dendritic remodeling, and trophic reward. Moreover, the authors hint at the potential to generalize this model to non-neural cells and tissues in the context of development and regeneration.In the introduction, the authors argue that theoretical and conceptual models of neural information processing are lagging behind empirical understanding. They highlight recent experimental findings, which demonstrate that dendrites (the branched extensions of neurons that receive signals from other neurons) undergo activity-dependent remodeling. This means that neurons aren't static structures but undergo continuous development throughout their lifecycle.The authors point out several implications of this perspective.
